languages">Indo-European</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em;padding-left:0"><li>
<a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">Insular Celtic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Brittonic_languages" title="Southwestern Brittonic languages">Southwestern Brittonic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><b>Breton</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Early forms</div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><div style="text-align:left;">Old Breton

50-ABB-ba to -be)</code></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Map showing the percentage of Breton speakers in each country of Brittany, 2018" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg/320px-Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg.png" decoding="async" width="320" height="247" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg/480px-Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg/640px-Breton_-_les_taux_de_locuteurs_selon_le_pays_-_sondage_2018.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="922" data-file-height="712" /></a></span><div style="text-align:left;">Percentage of Breton speakers in each country of Brittany, 2018</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><span class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Frameless"><a href="/wiki/File:Lang_Status_40-SE.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Lang_Status_40-SE.svg/320px-Lang_Status_40-SE.svg.png" decoding="async" width="320" height="96" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Lang_Status_40-SE.svg/480px-Lang_Status_40-SE.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Lang_Status_40-SE.svg/640px-Lang_Status_40-SE.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="153" /></a></span><div style="text-align:left;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; <p><b>Breton</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">BRET</span>-ən</i></a>, <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">French:</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">&#91;bʁətɔ̃&#93;</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Endonym" class="mw-redirect" title="Endonym">endonym</a>: <i lang="br">brezhoneg</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="br-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Breton" title="Help:IPA/Breton">&#91;bʁeˈzɔ̃ːnɛk&#93;</a></span> <span class="ext-phonos"><span data-nosnippet="" id="ooui-php-1" class="ext-phonos-PhonosButton noexcerpt ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-buttonElement oo-ui-buttonElement-frameless oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-buttonWidget" data-ooui="{&quot;_&quot;:&quot;mw.Phonos.PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;href&quot;:&quot;\/\/upload.wikimedia.org\/wikipedia\/commons\/transcoded\/a\/ac\/Br-brezhoneg.oga\/Br-brezhoneg.oga.mp3&quot;,&quot;rel&quot;:[&quot;nofollow&quot;],&quot;framed&quot;:false,&quot;icon&quot;:&quot;volumeUp&quot;,&quot;data&quot;:{&quot;ipa&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;lang&quot;:&quot;en&quot;,&quot;wikibase&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;file&quot;:&quot;Br-brezhoneg.oga&quot;},&quot;classes&quot;:[&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton&quot;,&quot;noexcerpt&quot;,&quot;ext-phonos-PhonosButton-emptylabel&quot;]}"><a role="button" tabindex="0" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/a/ac/Br-brezhoneg.oga/Br-brezhoneg.oga.mp3" rel="nofollow" aria-label="Play audio" title="Play audio" class="oo-ui-buttonElement-button"><span class="oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-volumeUp"></span><span class="oo-ui-labelElement-label"></span><span class="oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicatorElement-noIndicator"></span></a></span><sup class="ext-phonos-attribution noexcerpt navigation-not-searchable"><a href="/wiki/File:Br-brezhoneg.oga" title="File:Br-brezhoneg.oga">ⓘ</a></sup></span><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <small>or</small> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="br-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Breton" title="Help:IPA/Breton">&#91;brəhɔ̃ˈnek&#93;</a></span> <small>in <a href="/wiki/Morbihan" title="Morbihan">Morbihan</a></small>) is a <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Brittonic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Southwestern Brittonic language">Southwestern Brittonic language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic language group</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, part of modern-day France. It is the only Celtic language still widely in use on the European mainland, albeit as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_languages" title="Insular Celtic languages">insular</a> branch instead of the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Celtic_languages" title="Continental Celtic languages">continental</a> grouping.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Breton was brought from Great Britain to <a href="/wiki/Armorica" title="Armorica">Armorica</a> (the ancient name for the coastal region that includes the Brittany peninsula) by migrating <a href="/wiki/Britons_(Celtic_people)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (Celtic people)">Britons</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a>, making it an <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Insular Celtic language">Insular Celtic language</a>. Breton is most closely related to <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, another Southwestern Brittonic language.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> and the extinct <a href="/wiki/Cumbric_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Cumbric language">Cumbric</a>, both <a href="/wiki/Western_Brittonic_languages" title="Western Brittonic languages">Western Brittonic languages</a>, are more distantly related, and the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>) have a slight connection due to both of their origins being from Insular Celtic.
</p><p>Having declined from more than one million speakers around 1950 to about 200,000 in the first decade of the 21st century, Breton is classified as "severely endangered" by the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Book_of_Endangered_Languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Book of Endangered Languages">Atlas of the World's Languages in Danger</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-cite_UNESCO_Atlas_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cite_UNESCO_Atlas-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> However, the number of children attending bilingual classes rose 33% between 2006 and 2012 to 14,709.<sup id="cite_ref-Broudic2009_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broudic2009-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ofis-stats_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ofis-stats-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_status">History and status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History and status"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_boundary_of_Brittany" title="Linguistic boundary of Brittany">Linguistic boundary of Brittany</a></div>
<p>Breton is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Brittany" title="Lower Brittany">Lower Brittany</a> (<a class="mw-selflink selflink">Breton</a>: <i lang="br">Breizh-Izel</i>), roughly to the west of a line linking <a href="/wiki/Plouha" title="Plouha">Plouha</a> (west of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Brieuc" title="Saint-Brieuc">Saint-Brieuc</a>) and <a href="/wiki/La_Roche-Bernard" title="La Roche-Bernard">La Roche-Bernard</a> (east of <a href="/wiki/Vannes" title="Vannes">Vannes</a>). It comes from a Brittonic language community that once extended from Great Britain to <a href="/wiki/Armorica" title="Armorica">Armorica</a> (present-day Brittany) and had even established a toehold in <a href="/wiki/Galicia_(Spain)" title="Galicia (Spain)">Galicia</a> (in present-day Spain). Old Breton is attested from the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It was the language of the upper classes until the 12th century, after which it became the language of commoners in Lower Brittany. The nobility, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a>, adopted <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>. The written language of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brittany" title="Duchy of Brittany">Duchy of Brittany</a> was <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, switching to French in the 15th century. There exists a limited tradition of <a href="/wiki/Breton_literature" title="Breton literature">Breton literature</a>. Some <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> and scientific terms in Modern Breton come from Old Breton. The recognized stages of the Breton language are: <b>Old Breton</b> – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;800</span> to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1100</span>, <b>Middle Breton</b> – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1100</span> to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1650</span>, <b>Modern Breton</b> – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1650</span> to present.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The French monarchy was not concerned with the <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">minority</a> <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_France" title="Languages of France">languages of France</a>, spoken by the lower classes, and required the use of French for government business as part of its policy of national unity. During the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, the government introduced policies favouring French over the regional languages, which it pejoratively referred to as <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Patois" title="Patois">patois</a></i></span>. The revolutionaries assumed that <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monarchy" title="Monarchy">monarchist</a> forces preferred <a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">regional languages</a> to try to keep the peasant masses under-informed. In 1794, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Bar%C3%A8re" title="Bertrand Barère">Bertrand Barère</a> submitted his "report on the <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">patois</i></span>" to the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Public_Safety" title="Committee of Public Safety">Committee of Public Safety</a> in which he said that "federalism and superstition speak Breton".<sup id="cite_ref-breizh_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breizh-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>A Breton speaker, recorded in the United States.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/220px--WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="124" data-durationhint="407" data-mwtitle="WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.720p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="720p.vp9.webm" data-width="1280" data-height="720" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-width="1280" data-height="720" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.m3u8" type="application/vnd.apple.mpegurl" data-transcodekey="m3u8" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Iain_William_speaking_Breton.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>A Breton speaker, recorded in Canada.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Since the 19th century, under the <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">Third</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic" title="French Fourth Republic">Fourth</a> and now <a href="/wiki/French_Fifth_Republic" title="French Fifth Republic">Fifth Republics</a>, the French government has attempted to stamp out minority languages—including Breton—in state schools, in an effort to build a national culture. Teachers humiliated students for using their regional languages, and such practices prevailed until the late 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-breizh_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breizh-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 21st century, due to the political centralization of France, the influence of the media, and the increasing mobility of people, only about 200,000 people are active speakers of Breton, a dramatic decline from more than 1 million in 1950. The majority of today's speakers are more than 60 years old, and Breton is now classified as an <a href="/wiki/Endangered_language" title="Endangered language">endangered language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Broudic2009_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broudic2009-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the beginning of the 20th century, half of the population of Lower Brittany knew only Breton; the other half were bilingual. By 1950, there were only 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Monolingualism" title="Monolingualism">monolingual</a> Bretons, and this rapid decline has continued, with likely no monolingual speakers left today. A statistical survey in 1997 found around 300,000 speakers in Lower Brittany, of whom about 190,000 were aged 60 or older. Few 15- to 19-year-olds spoke Breton.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In 1993, parents were finally legally allowed to give their children Breton names.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Revival_efforts">Revival efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Revival efforts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Affiche_Charrue_Huard.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Affiche_Charrue_Huard.jpg/220px-Affiche_Charrue_Huard.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="315" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f2/Affiche_Charrue_Huard.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="430" /></a><figcaption>1911 poster with Breton slogan, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Burzudus eo!</i></span> ("It's miraculous!")</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1925, Professor <a href="/wiki/Roparz_Hemon" title="Roparz Hemon">Roparz Hemon</a> founded the Breton-language review <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Gwalarn" title="Gwalarn">Gwalarn</a></i></span>. During its 19-year run, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Gwalarn</i></span> tried to raise the language to the level of a great international language.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Its publication encouraged the creation of original literature in all genres, and proposed Breton translations of internationally recognized foreign works. In 1946, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Al_Liamm" title="Al Liamm">Al Liamm</a></i></span> replaced <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Gwalarn</i></span>. Other Breton-language periodicals have been published, which established a fairly large body of literature for a minority language.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1977, <a href="/wiki/Diwan_(school)" title="Diwan (school)">Diwan schools</a> were founded to teach Breton by <a href="/wiki/Language_immersion" title="Language immersion">immersion</a>. Since their establishment, Diwan schools have provided fully immersive primary school and partially immersive secondary school instruction in Breton for thousands of students across Brittany. This has directly contributed to the growing numbers of school-age speakers of Breton.
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Asterix" title="Asterix">Asterix</a></i> comic series has been translated into Breton. According to the comic, the <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaulish</a> village where Asterix lives is in the <a href="/wiki/Armorica" title="Armorica">Armorica peninsula</a>, which is now Brittany. Some other popular comics have also been translated into Breton, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">The Adventures of Tintin</a></i>, <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Spirou_et_Fantasio" class="mw-redirect" title="Spirou et Fantasio">Spirou</a></i></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Titeuf" title="Titeuf">Titeuf</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4gar_the_Horrible" title="Hägar the Horrible">Hägar the Horrible</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Peanuts" title="Peanuts">Peanuts</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Yakari" title="Yakari">Yakari</a></i>.
</p><p>Some original media are created in Breton. The sitcom, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Ken Tuch</i></span>, is in Breton.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radio_Kerne" title="Radio Kerne">Radio Kerne</a>, broadcasting from <a href="/wiki/Finist%C3%A8re" title="Finistère">Finistère</a>, has exclusively Breton programming. Some movies (<i><a href="/wiki/Lancelot_du_Lac_(film)" title="Lancelot du Lac (film)">Lancelot du Lac</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_in_Love" title="Shakespeare in Love">Shakespeare in Love</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Marion_du_Faouet&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Marion du Faouet (page does not exist)">Marion du Faouet</a></i>, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sezneg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Sezneg (page does not exist)">Sezneg</a></i>) and TV series (<i><a href="/wiki/Columbo_(TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbo (TV series)">Columbo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Perry_Mason_(1957_TV_series)" title="Perry Mason (1957 TV series)">Perry Mason</a></i>) have also been translated and broadcast in Breton. Poets, singers, linguists, and writers who have written in Breton, including <a href="/wiki/Yann-Ber_Kalloc%CA%BCh" title="Yann-Ber Kallocʼh">Yann-Ber Kallocʼh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roparz_Hemon" title="Roparz Hemon">Roparz Hemon</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Anjela_Duval&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Anjela Duval (page does not exist)">Anjela Duval</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xavier_de_Langlais" title="Xavier de Langlais">Xavier de Langlais</a>, <a href="/wiki/P%C3%AAr-Jakez_Helias" title="Pêr-Jakez Helias">Pêr-Jakez Helias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Youenn_Gwernig" title="Youenn Gwernig">Youenn Gwernig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glenmor" title="Glenmor">Glenmor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vefa_de_Saint-Pierre" title="Vefa de Saint-Pierre">Vefa de Saint-Pierre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stivell" title="Alan Stivell">Alan Stivell</a> are now known internationally.
</p><p>Today, Breton is the only living <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic language</a> that is not recognized by a national government as an official or regional language.
</p><p>The first Breton dictionary, the <i><a href="/wiki/Catholicon_(trilingual_dictionary)" title="Catholicon (trilingual dictionary)">Catholicon</a></i>, was also the first French dictionary. Edited by Jehan Lagadec in 1464,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> it was a trilingual work containing Breton, French and Latin. Today bilingual dictionaries have been published for Breton and languages including English, Dutch, German, Spanish and Welsh. A monolingual dictionary, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Geriadur Brezhoneg an Here</i></span> was published in 1995. The first edition contained about 10,000 words, and the second edition of 2001 contains 20,000 words.
</p><p>In the early 21st century, the <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Ofis_Publik_ar_Brezhoneg" title="Ofis Publik ar Brezhoneg">Ofis Publik ar Brezhoneg</a></i></span> ("Public Office for the Breton language") began a campaign to encourage daily use of Breton in the region by both businesses and local communes. Efforts include installing bilingual signs and posters for regional events, as well as encouraging the use of the <a href="/wiki/Spilhennig" title="Spilhennig">Spilhennig</a> to let speakers identify each other. The office also started an <a href="/wiki/Internationalization_and_localization" title="Internationalization and localization">Internationalization and localization</a> policy asking <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/SPIP" title="SPIP">SPIP</a> to develop their interfaces in Breton. In 2004, the <a href="/wiki/Breton_Wikipedia" title="Breton Wikipedia">Breton Wikipedia</a> started, which now counts more than 85,000 articles. In March 2007, the <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Ofis ar Brezhoneg</i></span> signed a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tripartite" class="extiw" title="wikt:tripartite">tripartite agreement</a> with <a href="/wiki/Regional_Council_of_Brittany" title="Regional Council of Brittany">Regional Council of Brittany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> for the consideration of the Breton language in Microsoft products. In October 2014, <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> added Breton as one of its 121 languages<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> after three years of talks between the <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Ofis</i></span> and Facebook.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/France_in_the_Eurovision_Song_Contest" title="France in the Eurovision Song Contest">France</a> has twice chosen to enter the <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest" title="Eurovision Song Contest">Eurovision Song Contest</a> with songs in Breton; once in <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_1996" title="Eurovision Song Contest 1996">1996</a> in Oslo with "<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Diwanit bugale</i></span>" by <a href="/wiki/Dan_Ar_Braz" title="Dan Ar Braz">Dan Ar Braz</a> and the fifty piece band <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A9ritage_des_Celtes" title="Héritage des Celtes">Héritage des Celtes</a>, and most recently in <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_2022" title="Eurovision Song Contest 2022">2022</a> in Turin with "<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Fulenn" title="Fulenn">Fulenn</a></i></span>" by <a href="/wiki/Alvan_(musician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alvan (musician)">Alvan Morvan Rosius</a> and vocal trio <a href="/wiki/Ahez" title="Ahez">Ahez</a>. These are two of five times France has chosen songs in one of its <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_France" title="Languages of France">minority languages</a> for the contest, the others being in <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_1992" title="Eurovision Song Contest 1992">1992</a> (bilingual French and <a href="/wiki/Antillean_Creole" title="Antillean Creole">Antillean Creole</a>), <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_1993" title="Eurovision Song Contest 1993">1993</a> (bilingual French and <a href="/wiki/Corsican_language" title="Corsican language">Corsican</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_2011" title="Eurovision Song Contest 2011">2011</a> (Corsican).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution_and_dialects">Geographic distribution and dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution and dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Breton_dialectes-en.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Breton_dialectes-en.svg/330px-Breton_dialectes-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="239" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Breton_dialectes-en.svg/495px-Breton_dialectes-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Breton_dialectes-en.svg/660px-Breton_dialectes-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="437" data-file-height="317" /></a><figcaption>Dialects of Breton</figcaption></figure>
<p>Breton is spoken mainly in Lower Brittany, but also in a more dispersed way in Upper Brittany (where it is spoken alongside <a href="/wiki/Gallo_language" title="Gallo language">Gallo</a> and French), and in areas around the world that have Breton emigrants.
</p><p>The four traditional dialects of Breton correspond to medieval bishoprics rather than to linguistic divisions. They are <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">leoneg</i></span> (<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">léonard</i></span>, of the <a href="/wiki/Viscounty_of_L%C3%A9on" title="Viscounty of Léon">county of Léon</a>), <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">tregerieg</i></span> (<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A9gorrois_Breton_dialect" title="Trégorrois Breton dialect">trégorrois</a></i></span>, of <a href="/wiki/Tr%C3%A9gor" title="Trégor">Trégor</a>), <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">kerneveg</i></span> (<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">cornouaillais</i></span>, of <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Cornouaille" title="Cornouaille">Cornouaille</a></i></span>), and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Gwenedeg" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwenedeg">gwenedeg</a></i></span> (<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">vannetais</i></span>, of <a href="/wiki/Vannes" title="Vannes">Vannes</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Guérandais</i></span> was spoken up to the beginning of the 20th century in the region of <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%A9rande" title="Guérande">Guérande</a> and <a href="/wiki/Batz-sur-Mer" title="Batz-sur-Mer">Batz-sur-Mer</a>. There are no clear boundaries between the dialects because they form a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>, varying only slightly from one village to the next.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Gwenedeg" class="mw-redirect" title="Gwenedeg">Gwenedeg</a></i></span>, however, requires a little study to be intelligible with most of the other dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-kergoat_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kergoat-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg/220px-Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="255" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg/330px-Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg/440px-Municipal_electronic_information_sign_in_Breton_in_Carhaix.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1107" data-file-height="1285" /></a><figcaption>Electronic information sign in Breton, <a href="/wiki/Carhaix" class="mw-redirect" title="Carhaix">Carhaix</a></figcaption></figure>

<td>4.6%
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Official status"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Language_policy_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Language policy of France">Language policy of France</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg/220px-Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg/330px-Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg/440px-Ofis_ar_Brezhoneg_vehicle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1516" data-file-height="1095" /></a><figcaption><span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Ofis_Publik_ar_Brezhoneg" title="Ofis Publik ar Brezhoneg">Ofis Publik ar Brezhoneg</a></i></span>, the Breton language agency, was set up in 1999 by the Brittany region to promote and develop the use of Breton.</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nation">Nation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Nation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As noted, only French is an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. Supporters of Breton and other minority languages continue to argue for their recognition, and for their place in education, public schools, and public life.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoopes_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoopes-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Constitution">Constitution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Constitution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In July 2008, the legislature amended the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_France" title="Constitution of France">French Constitution</a>, adding article 75-1: <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">les langues régionales appartiennent au patrimoine de la France</i></span> (the <a href="/wiki/Regional_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Regional languages">regional languages</a> belong to the heritage of <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>, which obliges signatory states to recognize minority and regional languages, was signed by France in 1999 but has not been ratified. On 27 October 2015, the <a href="/wiki/Senate_(France)" title="Senate (France)">Senate</a> rejected a draft constitutional law ratifying the charter.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg/220px-Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg/330px-Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg/440px-Bilingual_sin_in_Gwened.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a><figcaption>Bilingual sign in <a href="/wiki/Vannes" title="Vannes">Gwened</a>/Vannes</figcaption></figure>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Region">Region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Region"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Regional and departmental authorities use Breton to a very limited extent. Some bilingual signage has also been installed, such as street name signs in Breton towns. One station of the <a href="/wiki/Rennes_Metro" title="Rennes Metro">Rennes metro system</a> has signs in both French and Breton.
</p><p>Under the French law known as <a href="/wiki/Toubon_Law" title="Toubon Law">Toubon</a>, it is illegal for commercial signage to be in Breton alone. Signs must be bilingual or French only. Since commercial signage usually has limited physical space, most businesses have signs only in French.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Ofis_Publik_ar_Brezhoneg" title="Ofis Publik ar Brezhoneg">Ofis Publik ar Brezhoneg</a></i></span>, the Breton language agency, was set up in 1999 by the Brittany region to promote and develop the daily use of Breton.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> It helped to create the <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Ya_d%27ar_brezhoneg" title="Ya d&#39;ar brezhoneg">Ya d'ar brezhoneg</a></i></span> campaign, to encourage enterprises, organisations and communes to promote the use of Breton, for example by installing bilingual signage or translating their websites into Breton.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Education"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg/220px-Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg/330px-Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg/440px-Breton_school_sign_in_Rennes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="537" data-file-height="300" /></a><figcaption>Sign in French and partly in Breton in Rennes, outside a school with bilingual classes</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the late 20th century, the French government considered incorporating the independent Breton-language immersion schools (called <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Diwan_(school)" title="Diwan (school)">Diwan</a></i></span>) into the state education system. This action was blocked by the French <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Council_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitutional Council of France">Constitutional Council</a> based on the 1994 amendment to the Constitution that establishes French as the language of the republic. Therefore, no other language may be used as a language of instruction in state schools. The <a href="/wiki/Toubon_Law" title="Toubon Law">Toubon Law</a> implemented the amendment, asserting that French is the language of public education.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Diwan_(school)" title="Diwan (school)">Diwan schools</a> were founded in Brittany in 1977 to teach Breton by <a href="/wiki/Language_immersion" title="Language immersion">immersion</a>. Since their establishment, Diwan schools have provided fully immersive primary school and partially immersive secondary school instruction in Breton for thousands of students across Brittany. This has directly contributed to the growing numbers of school-age speakers of Breton. The schools have also gained fame from their high level of results in school exams, including those on French language and literature.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Breton-language schools do not receive funding from the national government, though the Brittany Region may fund them.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another teaching method is a bilingual approach by <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Div Yezh</i></span><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> ("Two Languages") in the State schools, created in 1979. <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Dihun</i></span><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> ("Awakening") was created in 1990 for bilingual education in the Catholic schools.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Statistics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2018, 18,337<sup id="cite_ref-ofis-stats_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ofis-stats-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> pupils (about 2.00% of all pupils in Brittany) attended <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Diwan</i></span>, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Div Yezh</i></span> and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Dihun</i></span> schools, and their number has increased yearly. The goal of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Yves_Le_Drian" title="Jean-Yves Le Drian">Jean-Yves Le Drian</a> (president of the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Council_of_Brittany" title="Regional Council of Brittany">Regional Council</a>) of 20,000, and of "their recognition" for "their place in education, public schools, and public life", by 2010, was not achieved, but he describes being encouraged by their progress.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, some 4,500 to 5,000 adults followed such a Breton language course as an evening or correspondence one.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="A course distributed by mail? Student writing as unmonitored practice? Perhaps with consistent feedback? With feedback based in formal standards?...from screened speakers? etc. (January 2020)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The transmission<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="within families? (June 2020)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> of Breton in 1999 is estimated to be 3 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-ofis-stats_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ofis-stats-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_forms_of_education">Other forms of education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other forms of education"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In addition to bilingual education (including Breton-medium education) the region has introduced the Breton language in primary education, mainly in the department of Finistère. These "initiation" sessions are generally one to three hours per week, and consist of songs and games.
</p><p>Schools in secondary education (<span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge" class="mw-redirect" title="Collège">collèges</a></i></span> and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br"><a href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e" class="mw-redirect" title="Lycée">lycées</a></i></span>) offer some courses in Breton. In 2010, nearly 5,000 students in Brittany were reported to be taking this option.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, the University of Rennes 2 has a Breton language department offering courses in the language along with a master's degree in Breton and Celtic Studies.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Vowels in Breton may be <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">short or long</a>. All unstressed vowels are short; stressed vowels can be short or long (vowel lengths are not noted in usual orthographies as they are implicit in the phonology of particular dialects, and not all dialects pronounce stressed vowels as long). An emergence of a schwa sound occurs as a result of vowel neutralization in post-tonic position, among different dialects.
</p><p>All vowels can also be <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">nasalized</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> which is noted by appending an 'n' letter after the base vowel, or by adding a combining tilde above the vowel (most commonly and easily done for <i>a</i> and <i>o</i> due to the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_orthography" title="Portuguese orthography">Portuguese letters</a>), or more commonly by non-ambiguously appending an <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;ñ&#x27e9;</span> letter after the base vowel (this depends on the orthographic variant).
</p>

<p>Diphthongs are <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ai,<span class="wrap"> </span>ei,<span class="wrap"> </span>ou/</span>.
</p>

<ul><li>The pronunciation of the letter <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;r&#x27e9;</span> varies nowadays: <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʁ]</span> is used in the French-influenced standard language and, generally speaking, in the central parts of Lower Brittany (including the south of Trégor, the west of Vannetais and virtually all parts of Cornouaille) whereas <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[r]</span> is the common realisation in Léon and often in the Haut-Vannetais dialect of central Morbihan (in and around the city of Vannes and the Pays de Pontivy), though in rapid speech mostly a tapped <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span> occurs. In the other regions of Trégor <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɾ]</span> or even <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɹ]</span> may be found.</li>
<li>The voiced dental fricative (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental_fricative" title="Voiced dental fricative">ð</a>/</span>) is a conservative realisation of the <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">lenition</a> (or the "spirant mutation" in cases where the phenomenon originates from the mutation of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t~θ/</span>, respectively) of the consonants <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> which is to be found in certain varieties of Haut-Vannetais. Most of the Breton dialects do not inherit the sound and thus it is mostly not orthographically fixed. The <i>Peurunvan</i>, for instance, uses <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;z&#x27e9;</span> for both mutations, which are regularly and more prominently pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[z]</span> in Léonais, Cornouaillais, Trégorrois and Bas-Vannetais. In traditional literature written in the Vannetais dialect, two different graphemes are employed for representing the dental fricative, depending on the scripture's historical period. There once was a time when <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;d&#x27e9;</span> was used to transcribe the sound, but today mostly the regular <span class="nowrap">&#x27e8;z&#x27e9;</span> is instead used, and this practice can be traced back to at least the end of the 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The area this phenomenon has been found to be evident in encompasses the towns of <a href="/wiki/Pontivy" title="Pontivy">Pontivy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baud,_Morbihan" title="Baud, Morbihan">Baud</a> and surrounding smaller villages like <a href="/wiki/Cl%C3%A9gu%C3%A9rec" title="Cléguérec">Cléguérec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noyal-Pontivy" title="Noyal-Pontivy">Noyal-Pontivy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plum%C3%A9liau" title="Pluméliau">Pluméliau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint-Allouestre" title="Saint-Allouestre">St. Allouestre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint-Barth%C3%A9lemy,_Morbihan" title="Saint-Barthélemy, Morbihan">St. Barthélemy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pluvigner" title="Pluvigner">Pluvigner</a> and also parts of <a href="/wiki/Belle_%C3%8Ele" title="Belle Île">Belle-Île</a>. The only known place where the mutation occurs outside of the Vannes country is the <a href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele_de_Sein" title="Île de Sein">Île de Sein</a>, an island located off <a href="/wiki/Finist%C3%A8re" title="Finistère">Finistère</a>'s coast. Some scholars also used <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ẓ]</span> as the symbol for the sound to indicate that it was rather an "infra-dental" consonant than a clear interdental, which is the sound the symbol <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ð/</span> is usually describes. Other linguists, however, did not draw that distinction, either because they identified the sound to actually be an interdental fricative (such as Roparz Hemon in his phonetic transcription of the dialect used in Pluméliau or Joseph Loth in his material about the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Sauzon" title="Sauzon">Sauzon</a> in Belle-Île) or due to the fact that they attached no importance to it and ascertained that their descriptions were not in need of a further clarification of the sound's phonetic realisation as it was a clearly distinguishable phoneme.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The digraph <i>zh</i> represents a variable sound that may exhibit as /s/, /z/, or /h/, and descends from a now-extinct sound <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span>, which is still extant in Welsh as <i>th</i>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Breton_grammar" title="Breton grammar">Breton grammar</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">Nouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Breton nouns are marked for gender and number. While Breton gender is fairly typical of gender systems across western Europe (with the exception of Basque and modern English), Breton number markers demonstrate rarer behaviors.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Gender"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Breton has two genders: masculine (<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">gourel</i></span>) and feminine (<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">gwregel</i></span>), having largely lost its historic neuter (<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">nepreizh</i></span>) as has also occurred in the other Celtic languages as well as across the Romance languages. Certain suffixes (<i>-ach/-aj,<sup id="cite_ref-BallGender_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallGender-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> -(a)dur,<sup id="cite_ref-BallGender_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallGender-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> -er, -lecʼh, -our, -ti, -va</i><sup id="cite_ref-Stephens_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>) are masculine, while others (<i>-enti, -er, -ez, -ezh, -ezon, -i</i>, <i>-eg</i>, <i>-ell</i>, and the singulative <i>-enn</i>) are feminine.<sup id="cite_ref-Stephens_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The suffix <i>-eg</i> can be masculine or feminine.<sup id="cite_ref-BallGender_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallGender-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are certain non-determinant factors that influence gender assignment. Biological sex is applied for animate referents. Metals, time divisions (except for <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">eur</i></span> "hour", <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">noz</i></span> "night" and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">sizhun</i></span> "week") and mountains tend to be masculine, while rivers, cities and countries tend to be feminine.<sup id="cite_ref-BallGender_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallGender-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, gender assignment to certain words often varies between dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-BallGender_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallGender-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Number">Number</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Number"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Number in Breton is primarily based on an opposition between singular and plural.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, the system is full of complexities<sup id="cite_ref-Fortson_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> in how this distinction is realized.
</p><p>Although modern Breton has lost its ancestral dual number marker, relics of its use are preserved in various nouns pertaining to body parts, including the words for eyes, ears, cheeks, legs, armpits, arms, hands, knees, thighs, and wings. This is seen in a prefix (formed in <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">daou</i></span>, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">di</i></span> or <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">div</i></span>) that is etymologically derived from the prefixation of the number two.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fortson_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The dual is no longer productive, and has merely been lexicalized in these cases rather than remaining a part of Breton grammar. The (etymologically) already dual words for eyes (<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">daoulagad</i></span>) and ears (<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">divskouarn</i></span>) can be pluralized "again" to form <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">daoulagad<b>où</b></i></span> and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">diskouarn<b>où</b></i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stephens_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stephens-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Like other Brythonic languages, Breton has a <a href="/wiki/Singulative" class="mw-redirect" title="Singulative">singulative</a> suffix that is used to form singulars out of <a href="/wiki/Collective_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Collective nouns">collective nouns</a>, for which the morphologically less complex form is the plural. Thus, the singulative of the collective <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">logod</i></span> "mice" is <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">logod<b>enn</b></i></span> "mouse".<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, Breton goes beyond Welsh in the complications of this system. Collectives can be pluralized to make forms which are different in meaning from the normal collective-- <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">pesk</i></span> "fish" (singular) is pluralized to <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">pesked</i></span>, singulativized to <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">peskedenn</i></span>, referring to a single fish out of a school of fish, and this singulative of the plural can then be pluralized again to make <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">peskedennoù</i></span> "fishes".<sup id="cite_ref-Fortson_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On top of this, the formation of plurals is complicated by two different pluralizing functions. The "default" plural formation is contrasted with another formation which is said to "emphasize variety or diversity" – thus two semantically different plurals can be formed out of <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">park</i></span>: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">parkoù</i></span> "parks" and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">parkeier</i></span> "various different parks".<sup id="cite_ref-Fortson_45-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Ball reports that the latter pluralizer is used only for inanimate nouns.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Certain formations have been lexicalized to have meanings other than that which might be predicted solely from the morphology: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">dour</i></span> "water" pluralized forms <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">dourioù</i></span> which means not "waters" but instead "rivers", while <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">doureier</i></span> now has come to mean "running waters after a storm". Certain forms have lost the singular from their paradigm: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">keloù</i></span> means "news" and <span title="Breton-language text">&#42;<i lang="br">kel</i></span> is not used, while <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">keleier</i></span> has become the regular plural,<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> 'different news items'.
</p><p>Meanwhile, certain nouns can form doubly marked plurals with lexicalized meanings – <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bugel</i></span> "child" is pluralized once into <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bugale</i></span> "children" and then pluralized a second time to make <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bugaleoù</i></span> "groups of children".<sup id="cite_ref-Fortson_45-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The diminutive suffix <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ig</i></span> also has the somewhat unusual property of triggering double marking of the plural: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bugelig</i></span> means "little child", but the doubly pluralized <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bug<b>ale</b>ig<b>où</b></i></span> means "little children"; <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bag</i></span> boat has a singular diminutive <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bagig</i></span> and a simple plural <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bagoù</i></span>, thus its diminutive plural is the doubly pluralized <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bag<b>où</b>ig<b>où</b></i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fortson_45-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fortson-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As seen elsewhere in many Celtic languages, the formation of the plural can be hard to predict, being determined by a mix of semantic, morphological and lexical factors.
</p><p>The most common plural marker is <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-où</i></span>, with its variant <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ioù</i></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> most nouns that use this marker are inanimates but collectives of both inanimate and animate nouns always use it as well.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Most animate nouns, including trees, take a plural in <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ed</i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> However, in some dialects the use of this affix has become rare. Various masculine nouns including occupations as well as the word <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Saoz</i></span> ("Englishman", plural <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">Saozon</i></span>) take the suffix <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ien</i></span>, with a range of variants including <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-on</i></span>, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ion</i></span>, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-an</i></span> and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ian</i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The rare pluralizing suffixes <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-er</i></span>/<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ier</i></span> and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-i</i></span> are used for a few nouns. When they are appended, they also trigger a change in the vowel of the root: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-i</i></span> triggers a <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a> effect whereby some or all preceding vowels are changed to <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">i</i></span> (<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">kenderv</i></span> "cousin" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">kindirvi</i></span> "cousins"; <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">bran</i></span> "crow" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">brini</i></span> "crows"; <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">klujur</i></span> "partridge" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">klujiri</i></span> "partridges"); the changes associated with <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-er</i></span>/<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">-ier</i></span> are less predictable.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Various nouns instead form their plural merely with <a href="/wiki/Ablaut" class="mw-redirect" title="Ablaut">ablaut</a>: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">a</i></span> or <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">o</i></span> in the stem being changed to <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">e</i></span>: <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">askell</i></span> "wing" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">eskell</i></span> "wings"; <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">dant</i></span> "tooth" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">dent</i></span> "teeth"; <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">kordenn</i></span> "rope" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">kerdenn</i></span> "ropes".<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another set of nouns have lexicalized plurals that bear little if any resemblance to their singulars. These include <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">placʼh</i></span> "girl" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">mercʼhed</i></span>, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">porcʼhell</i></span> "pig" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">mocʼh</i></span>, <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">buocʼh</i></span> "cow" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">saout</i></span>, and <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">ki</i></span> "dog" → <span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">chas</i></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In compound nouns, the head noun, which usually comes first, is pluralized.<sup id="cite_ref-BallNumber_44-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallNumber-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbal_aspect">Verbal aspect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Verbal aspect"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As in other Celtic languages as well as <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, a variety of verbal constructions is available to express <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">grammatical aspect</a>, for example: showing a distinction between <a href="/wiki/Progressive_tense" class="mw-redirect" title="Progressive tense">progressive</a> and habitual actions:
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inflected_prepositions">Inflected prepositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Inflected prepositions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As in other modern <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic languages</a>, Breton pronouns are fused into preceding prepositions to produce a sort of <a href="/wiki/Inflected_preposition" title="Inflected preposition">inflected preposition</a>. Below are some examples in Breton, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>, along with English translations.
</p>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><div class="interlinear"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ur</p><p style="margin: 0px;">a</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">cʼharr</p><p style="margin: 0px;">car</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">zo</p><p style="margin: 0px;">is</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;"><b>ganimp</b></p><p style="margin: 0px;"><b>with-us</b></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">(or '<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">ganeomp</i></span>')</p><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p></div><p style="display: none;">ur cʼharr zo <b>ganimp</b></p><p style="display: none;">a car is <b>with-us</b></p><p class="mw-empty-elt"></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
</td>
<td><span title="Cornish-language text"><i lang="kw">yma karr <b>genen</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mae car <b>gennym</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Irish-language text"><i lang="ga">tá gluaisteán / carr <b>againn</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Scottish Gaelic-language text"><i lang="gd">tha càr <b>againn</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Manx-language text"><i lang="gv">ta gleashtan / carr <b>ain</b></i></span>
</td>
<td>we have a car
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><div class="interlinear"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">un</p><p style="margin: 0px;">a</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ti</p><p style="margin: 0px;">house</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">zo</p><p style="margin: 0px;">is</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;"><b>ganeocʼh</b></p><p style="margin: 0px;"><b>with-you.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="plural number" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PL</abbr></b></p></div><p style="display: none;">un ti zo <b>ganeocʼh</b></p><p style="display: none;">a house is <b>with-you.PL</b></p><p class="mw-empty-elt"></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
</td>
<td><span title="Cornish-language text"><i lang="kw">yma chi <b>genowgh</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mae tŷ <b>gennych</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Irish-language text"><i lang="ga">tá teach <b>agaibh</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Scottish Gaelic-language text"><i lang="gd">tha taigh <b>agaibh</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Manx-language text"><i lang="gv">ta thie <b>eu</b></i></span>
</td>
<td>you have a house
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><div class="interlinear"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">arcʼhant</p><p style="margin: 0px;">money</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">zo</p><p style="margin: 0px;">is</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p lang="br" style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;"><b>ganto</b></p><p style="margin: 0px;"><b>with-them</b></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;">(or '<span title="Breton-language text"><i lang="br">gante</i></span>')</p><p style="margin: 0px;">&#160;</p></div><p style="display: none;">arcʼhant zo <b>ganto</b></p><p style="display: none;">money is <b>with-them</b></p><p class="mw-empty-elt"></p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
</td>
<td><span title="Cornish-language text"><i lang="kw">yma mona <b>gansa</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mae arian <b>ganddynt</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Irish-language text"><i lang="ga">tá airgead <b>acu</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Scottish Gaelic-language text"><i lang="gd">tha airgead <b>aca</b></i></span>
</td>
<td><span title="Manx-language text"><i lang="gv">ta argid <b>oc</b></i></span>
</td>
<td>they have money
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Note that in the examples above the <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic languages</a> (Irish, Scottish Gaelic, and Manx) use the preposition meaning <i>at</i> to show possession, whereas the <a href="/wiki/Brittonic_languages" title="Brittonic languages">Brittonic languages</a> use <i>with</i>. The Goidelic languages, however, do use the preposition <i>with</i> to express "belong to" (Irish <span title="Irish-language text"><i lang="ga">is liom an leabhar</i></span>, Scottish <span title="Scottish Gaelic-language text"><i lang="gd">is leam an leabhar</i></span>, Manx <span title="Manx-language text"><i lang="gv">s'lhiams yn lioar</i></span>, The book belongs to me).
</p><p>The Welsh examples are in <a href="/wiki/Literary_Welsh_morphology#Prepositions" title="Literary Welsh morphology">literary Welsh</a>. The order and preposition may differ slightly in <a href="/wiki/Colloquial_Welsh_morphology#Prepositions" title="Colloquial Welsh morphology">colloquial Welsh</a> (Formal <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mae car gennym</i></span>, North Wales <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mae gynnon ni gar</i></span>, South Wales <span title="Welsh-language text"><i lang="cy">mae car gyda ni</i></span>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_consonant_mutations">Initial consonant mutations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Breton_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Initial consonant mutations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Breton_mutations" title="Breton mutations">Breton mutations</a></div>
<p>Breton has four initial <a href="/wiki/Consonant_mutation" title="Consonant mutation">consonant mutations</a>: though modern Breton lost the nasal mutation of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> (but for rare words such the word "door": "dor" "an nor"), it also has a "hard" mutation, in which voiced stops become voiceless, and a "mixed" mutation, which is a mixture of hard and soft mutations.
</p>
